Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



I. PROJECT SCOPE / LOCATION

   The Township of Maplewood recognizes the importance of a safe and efficient
   roadway system for continued economic vitality and the quality of life desired by its
   citizens. Therefore, the Township conducted a traffic survey via the compilation of a
   questionnaire disseminated to various stakeholders within the Township to identify
   locations throughout the Township in need of traffic calming measures and/or traffic
   safety improvements. The intent of the Township's survey was to obtain valuable
   input from key stakeholders, local organizations and committees to assist in the
   identification of these locations, their respective deficiencies and possible corrective
   measures. The key objectives of the aforementioned survey and this subsequent
   study are as follows:

   ·   Identify locations, along with their respective deficiencies, within the Township in
       need of traffic calming measures and/or traffic safety improvements.
   ·   Identify possible corrective measures to address the identified deficiencies.
   ·   Involve community stakeholders in the identification of deficiencies and possible
       corrective measures and throughout the entire study, design and construction
       process.
   ·   Provide corrective measures that are pedestrian and bicyclist friendly.
   ·   Provide corrective measures that are context sensitive and contribute to the
       quality of life of the adjoining community.
   ·   Coordinate with other municipal departments such as fire, police, emergency and
       parks and non-municipal agencies such as NJ Transit to allow for the proper
       access and circulation of their vehicles.
   ·   Avoid relocating the traffic problem to another street or section of the Township.
   ·   Ensure that the plan is compatible with other plans within the Township.
   ·   Develop a plan that is cost effective and results in minimal physical impact to the
       surrounding areas.
   The following locations were identified from the aforementioned traffic survey, as
   compiled by the Maplewood Township Engineer, as in need of traffic calming
   measures, traffic safety improvements, or as otherwise significant crash generators
   and/or problematic:

   ¾ Intersections
     1. C.R. 638, Valley Street, and Parker Avenue
     2. Jefferson Avenue and Maplewood Avenue
     3. Jefferson Avenue and Woodland Road / Walton Road
     4. Prospect Street and Elmwood Avenue
     5. C.R. 638, Valley Street, and Jefferson Avenue
     6. Ridgewood Road and Cedar Lane/E. Cedar Lane

   ¾ Roadways
     1. Ridgewood Road
     2. Boyden Avenue
     3. Prospect Street
     4. Parker Avenue

   In an effort to refine the scope, verify the traffic concerns as identified in the traffic
   survey and to discuss potential traffic calming measures and/or traffic safety
   improvements, a field meeting was held on Thursday, February 22, 2007 with
   representatives of the Maplewood Township Engineer's Office and Keller and
   Kirkpatrick. The following are the key issues discussed and the conclusions reached
   during the meeting:

   1. C.R. 638, Valley Street, and Parker Avenue

       ·   The attendees observed motorists, traveling either north or south along Valley
           Street, passing left turning vehicles on the right thereby creating a "shadowing
           effect".
       ·   The Maplewood Township Engineer recommended the installation of bump-
           outs to narrow the travel lanes along Valley Street and Parker Avenue to
           reduce the potential for motorists passing left turning vehicles on the right and
           thereby reduce the number of sideswipe crashes. It was added that the
           bump-outs would reduce pedestrian crossing times.
       ·   It was recommended that the existing traffic signal trombone mast arms and
           eight-inch signal heads be replaced with standard traffic signal mast arms and
           twelve-inch signal heads to increase signal visibility and thereby reduce the
           number of rear end crashes.


Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                     3
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       ·   It was observed that some of the existing pedestrian signal heads were not
           functioning and that others had poor visibility. In addition, it was noted that
           pedestrian signal heads were not present in the northeast quadrant of the
           intersection. It was recommended that countdown pedestrian signals be
           installed in the northeast quadrant and that all of the existing pedestrian
           signal heads be replaced with countdown pedestrian signal heads to increase
           pedestrian expectancy.
       ·   It was suggested that an exclusive left turn lane be added along the
           westbound approach of Parker Avenue due to the significant left turn volume
           along this approach. It was stated that the addition of an exclusive left turn
           lane along this approach would require widening along the eastbound
           approach along which there are physical constraints preventing such a
           widening. The addition of an exclusive left turn lane would also increase the
           pedestrian crossing time and that the intersection currently operates at an
           acceptable LOS during each of the peak hours.
       ·   Subsequent to the meeting it was observed that pedestrians cross at the
           unsignalized intersection of Valley Street and Cromwell Place, which is
           located approximately 420 feet to the south of the signalized intersection of
           Valley Street and Parker Avenue. While pedestrian handicap ramps exist at
           the intersection of Valley Street and Cromwell Place, there are no marked
           crosswalks.

   2. Prospect Street and Elmwood Avenue

       ·   The attendees observed that intersection sight distance from Elmwood
           Avenue is obstructed by vegetation located within the sight triangles and the
           profile of Prospect Street south of Elmwood Avenue. The attendees agreed
           that the sight triangles should be cleared of all obstructions.
       ·   To enhance pedestrian safety, it was recommended that "ladder" striping be
           added to the crosswalks to increase their visibility, additional warning signs be
           installed along the approach roadways to provide advance warning to
           motorists and that bump-outs be provided to reduce pedestrian crossing
           times and narrow the roadway width of Prospect Street in an attempt to
           reduce speeds along the same.

   3. C.R. 638, Valley Street, and Jefferson Avenue

       ·   It was stated that based on the recently collected traffic volumes and crash
           data that a traffic signal is not warranted at this intersection.
       ·   It was noted that the intersection sight distance from Jefferson Avenue was
           limited due to parking on Valley Street and existing vegetation. The
           attendees agreed that the sight triangles should be cleared of all obstructions.
       ·   It was recommended to install of bump-outs in both the southwest and
           northwest corners of the intersection to reduce pedestrian crossing times and
           allow for the relocation of the stop line along Jefferson Avenue further to the
           east thereby increasing intersection sight distance.

Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                    4
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       ·   The Maplewood Township Engineer suggested that the existing near side NJ
           Transit bus stop located along Valley Street southbound, north of Jefferson
           Avenue, be relocated south of Jefferson Avenue as a far side bus stop. It
           was noted that this would require the elimination of existing on-street parking
           along Valley Street in front of local businesses. It was added, however, that
           these businesses have off-street parking behind their buildings.
       ·   It was observed that the street name signing for Jefferson Avenue was
           obscure. It was recommended that either the street name signing be
           enhanced or that advanced warning signs for Jefferson Avenue be installed
           along Valley Street.
       ·   It was agreed by the attendees that the implementation of additional
           pedestrian safety measures need to be investigated including modified
           crosswalks and signing for enhanced visibility and advanced warning for
           motorists.

   4. Ridgewood Road and Cedar Lane/E. Cedar Lane

       ·   It was stated that based on the recently collected traffic volumes and crash
           data that a four-way stop is not warranted at this intersection.
       ·   The attendees observed that in addition to the existing buildings, illegally
           parked vehicles along Ridgewood Road northbound, north of Cedar Lane,
           were limiting the available intersection sight distance from Cedar Lane.
           Subsequent to the meeting bump-outs and the widening of the existing
           sidewalk in front of the local businesses located along Ridgewood Road
           northbound, north of Cedar Lane, were also discussed as viable options.

   5. Kensington Terrace

       ·   The traffic survey identified Kensington Terrace as a bypass for Valley Street
           and that speeding frequently occurs along Kensington Terrace.
       ·   It was suggested to allow school parking along Kensington Terrace to narrow
           the travel lanes and thereby discourage the use of Kensington Terrace as a
           bypass route. It was stated that residents do not want parking along this
           street due to students loitering in the area.
       ·   It was suggested to install bump-outs or speed tables along Kensington
           Terrace and/or install a traffic circle at the intersection of Kensington Terrace
           and Edgewood Road to reduce travel speeds and discourage the use of
           Kensington Terrace as a bypass route.

   6. Ridgewood Road / Prospect Street / Boyden Avenue

       ·   The attendees discussed such options as the addition of parking and/or bike
           lanes, bump-outs and traffic circles to reduce the travel speed along these
           roadways. It was also suggested that a raised median could be installed
           along Prospect Street to provide a boulevard effect, which may reduce the
           travel speed and would provide for a pedestrian refuge.

Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                    5
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



        ·   It was recommended that extension track striping be installed at the
            intersection of Boyden Avenue and Elmwood Avenue to direct motorists
            through the intersection.

   7. Baker Street

        ·   The traffic survey identified speeding and limited visibility along Baker Street,
            resulting from the underpass, combined with heavy pedestrian traffic as
            hazards along this roadway between Maplewood Avenue and Valley Street.
        ·   It was stated that the crosswalks across Baker Street between Dunnell Road
            and Burnett Street are in poor condition and that "ladder" striping, advance
            warning signs, bump-outs and/or raised crosswalks should be considered to
            enhance pedestrian safety and increase driver awareness of these pedestrian
            crossings.

   8.   Jacoby Street

        ·   The traffic survey identified Jacoby Street as a bypass between Springfield
            Avenue and Boyden Avenue. Limited sight distance and speeding were also
            noted along Jacoby Street. Mr. Calbi stated that 44th street is also used as a
            bypass to get to Chancellor Avenue.
        ·   It was suggested that 44th Street could be made a one-way street towards
            Chancellor Avenue or that a gate could be constructed across 44th Street and
            its intersection with Chancellor Avenue, essentially making 44th Street a dead
            end street.
        ·   It was suggested to make Jacoby Street a right-in, right-out only by
            constructing a divisional island along Springfield Avenue.

   9.   Sommer Avenue, Hubert Place, Osborne Terrace and Girard Place

        ·   It was suggested to create a one-way street grid in this area to discourage the
            use of these roadways as bypass routes. It was also suggested to install
            speed tables along Sommer Avenue.
        ·   It was agreed by the attendees that bump-outs and traffic circles should be
            investigated to reduce the travel speeds along these roadways and
            discourage their use as bypass routes.

   A copy of the minutes of this meeting can be found in Appendix A.

II. TRAFFIC CALMING MEASURES

   The purpose of traffic calming is to reduce the speed and/or volume of traffic to
   "acceptable" levels for the functional classification of the street and the nature of the
   bordering land use while maintaining an overall efficient transportation system. By
   addressing high speeds and cut-through volumes, traffic calming can increase both
   real and perceived safety of pedestrians and bicyclists, and improve the quality of
   life within a neighborhood.

   However, traffic calming measures also need to be designed and implemented such
   as to minimize impacts to emergency service vehicles, access to residences and
   local businesses, and snow removal operations. In addition the design of the
   measures must consider existing drainage patterns so as to avoid ice/water
   accumulation on a pedestrian crossing or roadway. Traffic calming measures also
   must be designed to meet the requirements set forth in the Americans with
   Disabilities Act.

   Based on the input received via the traffic survey conducted by the Township, the
   most prevalent complaint was that of speed. Although most traffic calming
   measures have some effect on both volume and speed, they are usually classified
   according to their dominant effect. Three types of traffic calming measures are
   utilized to control speed as classified in the ITE publication Traffic Calming: State of
   the Practice: vertical measures, which use forces of vertical acceleration to
   discourage speeding (i.e. speed tables, raised intersections); horizontal measures,
   which use forces of lateral acceleration to discourage speeding (i.e. traffic circles,
   chicanes); and narrowing, which use a psychological-perceptive sense of enclosure
   to discourage speeding (i.e. bump-outs, center islands). The following describes the
   traffic calming measures investigated for the various locations included in this study
   along with the pros and cons of each measure. It should be noted that all of the
   following traffic calming measures with the exception of raised intersections and
   speed cushions can be emulated on a temporary basis with barrels, cones and/or
   "rubber" curbing to determine their effectiveness prior to permanent installation.

   1. Bump-outs, also known as
      neckdowns, are a form of roadway
      narrowing that utilizes curb
      extensions at intersections or mid-
      block locations with significant
      pedestrian activity to reduce the
      roadway width as a means to
      achieve reductions in speed.
      When combined with crosswalks,
      they are sometimes referred to as
      safe crosses.     Their effect on
      vehicle speeds can be limited by
      the absence of pronounced vertical or horizontal deflection. Instead, their
      primary purpose is to enhance pedestrian safety. They do this by shortening the
      crossing distances for pedestrians and drawing attention to pedestrians via

Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                    7
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       raised peninsulas. The curb radii at the corners of the intersection are tightened,
       which reduces the speeds of turning vehicles. Bump-outs are also effective at
       improving intersection sight distance by allowing the side street stop lines to
       move closer to the main roadway. Figure 3 on page 13 shows typical bump-out
       configurations.

       Pros:
       · Improves pedestrian safety via reduced crossing distances and increased
          visibility for and of the pedestrian.
       · May reduce travel speed for through vehicles.
       · Reduces travel speed for turning vehicles.
       · Prevents illegal parking adjacent to intersection, which can reduce
          intersection sight distance and visibility for and of pedestrians.
       · Increases intersection sight distance.
       · Can provide areas for aesthetic enhancements.

       Cons:
       · Reduction in vehicular speed is limited compared to other methods since
         there is no pronounced vertical or horizontal deflection.
       · May result in the loss of on-street parking depending on the length of the
         bump-out, although this is unlikely given statutory prohibitions of parking
         close to intersections.
       · May limit ability of larger vehicles to turn right without crossing centerline of
         opposing roadway.
       · Difficult to accommodate full bicycle lanes.
       · May require modifications to existing stormwater management facilities.
       · Provisions need to be made for snow and ice removal.


   2. Traffic Circles are raised islands
      located in the center of unsignalized
      intersections around which traffic
      circulates. They prevent drivers from
      speeding by impeding the straight-
      through movement and by forcing
      drivers to slow down to yield. Traffic
      circles are usually landscaped and
      are typically controlled by a yield sign
      on each approach, to allow
      continuous movement within the
      circle.      Although effective, large
      vehicles, such as emergency vehicles, trucks and buses, can have difficulty
      negotiating the circle. This problem can be alleviated by performing the
      appropriate turning analysis for the design vehicle and making the traffic circles
      partially or wholly mountable. Figure 4 on page 14 shows a typical layout for a
      traffic circle at a four-way intersection. It should be noted that traffic circles differ

Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                       8
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       from roundabouts in that roundabouts are typically used on higher volume streets
       to allocate right-of-way to vehicles rather than using a traffic signal or all-way
       stop. Roundabouts are larger than traffic circles and typically include splitter
       islands to channel approaching traffic to the right.

       Pros:
       · Reduces travel speed for through vehicles.
       · Reduces the number of potential conflict points at an intersection.
       · Can significantly reduce collisions, particularly right angle collisions.
       · Can provide an area for aesthetic enhancements.

       Cons:
       · Large vehicles, such as emergency vehicles, trucks and buses, can have
         difficulty negotiating the circle if it is not at least partially mountable.
       · The horizontal deflection that occurs at circles may force vehicles into
         pedestrian crossing areas or into the travel paths of cyclists.
       · May require the removal of some on-street parking. Pennsylvania's Traffic
         Calming Handbook recommends the prohibition of parking for 30 feet from
         the intersection.
       · Provisions need to be made for snow and ice removal.


   3. Chicanes are curb extensions that
      alternate from one side of the street
      to the other, forming S-shaped
      curves. They are often grouped in a
      series of three curb extensions
      staggered on alternating sides of
      the street. By forcing motorists to
      weave through the extensions,
      chicanes reduce vehicular speeds.
      Chicanes should be designed so
      that vehicles cannot cut straight
      paths across the center line in an
      attempt to avoid the curvature of the roadway. Center islands have been added
      in some instances to prevent drivers from performing such movements. A
      chicane-like effect can also be achieved, at a fraction of the cost, by alternating
      on-street parking, if demand is present, from one side of the street to the other.
      Chicanes are not appropriate along streets with a high percentage of truck traffic.
      Figure 5 on page 15 shows a typical layout for a chicane.

       Pros:
       · Reduces travel speed for through vehicles, when designed appropriately.
       · May reduce traffic noise due to lower speeds and possibly volume.
       · Can provide areas for aesthetic enhancements.


Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                 9
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       Cons:
       · With two-lane chicanes, motorists may attempt to increase travel speeds by
         crossing the centerline to maintain a straight line of travel if not used in
         conjunction with center islands.
       · May impact driveways.
       · May require the elimination of on-street parking.
       · May require modifications to existing stormwater management facilities.


   4. Center Islands are basically a
      raised median located along the
      centerline of a roadway, which
      serve to narrow the travel lanes
      along the roadway.          Center
      islands may be more effective
      when they are installed as short
      interruptions to an otherwise open
      street cross section, rather than
      long     median     islands    that
      channelize traffic and separate
      opposing flows. The latter have
      been found to sometimes result in increased running speeds, while the former,
      perhaps because they appear as obstacles to approaching traffic, reportedly
      result in slower traffic. Center Islands are often nicely landscaped to provide
      visual amenity and can help pedestrianize streets by providing a refuge for
      pedestrians. Figure 5 on page 15 shows a typical center island improvement.

       Pros:
       · Reduces travel speed for through vehicles, when designed appropriately.
       · Provides a pedestrian refuge, thereby enhancing pedestrian safety.
       · Can provide areas for aesthetic enhancements.
       · Can be used to reduce cross-over collisions.
       · Can be used to control access.

       Cons:
       · May require extensive modifications to existing stormwater management
         facilities due to the reduction in available area for spread.
       · May require the elimination of on-street parking.
       · Will restrict access to and from driveways from one direction.


   5. Mid-block Narrowing, also known as chokers, are curb extensions at mid-block
      locations that narrow a street by widening the sidewalk or planting strip. If
      combined with crosswalks, they are sometimes referred to as safe crosses which
      are generally used at locations with significant pedestrian activity, school children
      or senior citizens. Roadway narrowing has been shown to provide a perception

Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                   10
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       of enclosure to the motorist, which
       in turn reduces speed. Figure 5 on
       page 15 indicates a typical mid-
       block narrowing improvement.

       Pros:
       · May reduce travel speed for
          through vehicles.
       · Improves pedestrian safety via
          reduced crossing times and increased visibility for and of the pedestrian.
       · Can provide areas for aesthetic enhancements.

       Cons:
       · Reduction in vehicle speed is limited compared to other methods since there
         is no pronounced vertical or horizontal deflection.
       · May require the elimination of on-street parking.
       · Difficult to accommodate full bicycle lanes.
       · May require modifications to existing stormwater management facilities.


   6. Raised Intersections are flat raised
      areas covering entire intersections,
      including crosswalks, with ramps on
      all approaches. The intersection is
      typically raised three to six inches
      above street level and often includes
      brick or other textured materials on
      the flat section.     They are not
      generally raised to sidewalk level so
      as to provide a "lip" for the visually
      impaired.       Raised intersections
      reduce vehicle speeds on all approaches and help to decrease conflicts between
      vehicles and pedestrians by delineating crossing areas and elevating pedestrians
      above street level. Typical locations for this type of traffic calming measure
      include business districts, local streets and collectors, or any location with high
      pedestrian activity. They are particulary useful in areas where the loss of on-
      street parking associated with other traffic calming measures is considered
      unacceptable. Figure 6 on page 16 indicates a typical raised intersection
      improvement.

       Pros:
       · Reduces travel speed along all approaches.
       · Improves pedestrian safety via increased visibility for and of the pedestrian.
       · Can provide opportunities for aesthetic enhancements.



Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                 11
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       Cons:
       · Reduction in vehicle speed is limited compared to other methods because of
         their long, flat top and gently sloped ramps.
       · Will reduce response times for emergency services.
       · A noted limitation to textured pavements, bricks and cobblestones is that they
         may present difficulties for pedestrians and bicyclists, particulary in wet
         conditions.
       · May require modifications to existing stormwater management facilities.
       · A measure such as flexible delineators may be required at the installation to
         alert snowplow operators.


   7. Speed Cushions are similar to speed
      tables, except that speed cushions are
      used to slow passenger vehicles without
      reducing response times for emergency
      services.    Speed cushions, which are
      generally three to four inches in height and
      12 to 22 feet in length, are typically used
      on residential roadways to slow vehicles to
      between approximately 15 and 25 mph.
      Speed cushions are designed as three or
      more small speed tables, which force
      passenger vehicles to slow down with one
      or both wheels on the cushion, but are
      spaced to allow wider axle vehicles, such
      as fire trucks, to straddle the speed
      cushions without delaying their response
      time. A typical installation is shown in Figure 7 on page 17. It should be noted
      that the number of speed cushions utilized per installation depends on the
      roadway width. In general, speed cushions should not be placed within 150 feet
      of an unsignalized intersection or 250 feet of a signalized intersection. Speed
      bumps, which are generally encountered in parking lots, are usually three to six
      inches in height and one to three feet in length and were not considered for this
      study since they may generate severe vertical displacement at low speeds.

       Pros:
       · Reduces travel speed.
       · Would not reduce response times for emergency services.
       · Relatively low cost to install and maintain.

       Cons:
       · A measure such as flexible delineators may be required at the installation to
         alert snowplow operators.
       · Could be confusing for bicyclists to navigate.
       · May require modifications to existing stormwater management facilities.

Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                               12
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



III. INTERSECTIONS

   1. C.R. 638, Valley Street, and Parker Avenue: This intersection, which is
      adjacent to Columbia High School, was identified in the traffic survey as having
      capacity issues during peak commuting hours and during periods of entry and
      dismissal for the high school. In addition, the survey identified significant
      pedestrian activity, specifically schoolchildren, associated with the crosswalks at
      this intersection.

       Existing Conditions: C.R. 638,
       Valley Street, in the vicinity of
       Parker Avenue is an undivided
       two-lane urban minor arterial
       running south to north with an
       approximate roadway width of
       30 feet. Parker Avenue in the
       vicinity of Valley Street is an
       undivided       two-lane    urban
       collector running west to east
       with an approximate roadway                               Pa
                                                                   rk e
       width of 30 feet and 34 feet west                               rA
                                                                          ven
                                                         et

       and east of Valley Street,                                             ue
                                                       re
                                                    St



       respectively. Valley Street has a
                                                   y




       posted speed limit of 35 mph
                                                 lle
                                               Va




       and is under the jurisdiction of
       Essex County. Parker Avenue has a posted speed limit of 25 mph and is under
       the jurisdiction of Maplewood Township.

       Valley Street and Parker Avenue form a four-legged intersection. All of the
       approaches to the intersection consist of a left/through/right lane with one
       receiving lane. The intersection is controlled by a two-phase, fixed-time traffic
       signal operating on a 70-second cycle length. Crosswalks with "ladder" striping
       currently exist across each of the approaches to the intersection. (Note: The
       "ladder" striping was installed in the crosswalks subsequent to the attached
       aerial.) Pedestrian signals exist in each quadrant of the intersection except for
       the northeast quadrant. A copy of the existing signal timing directive can be
       found in Appendix B. A S1-1, School Crossing, sign with a supplemental W16-
       7p, Diagonal Arrow, sign is posted along both of the Valley Street approaches at
       the intersection. All of the approaches are currently posted with a R10-11A, No
       Turn on Red, sign either overhead or ground mounted. Trucks over six tons
       traveling westbound on Parker Avenue are restricted from turning left at the
       intersection. The intersection is illuminated with a single luminaire mounted on a
       utility pole in the southwest quadrant of the intersection. Sidewalks currently
       exist along both sides of Valley Street and Parker Avenue.

       According to Maplewood Township ordinance, no vehicle shall be parked on any
       thoroughfare or public parking lot between the hours of 2:00 A.M. and 6:00 A.M.

Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                 18
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       Parking is prohibited at all times along Valley Street northbound from Crowell
       Place to a point 120 feet south of Parker Avenue and from Parker Avenue to a
       point 120 feet south of Hixon Place. In addition, parking is prohibited at all times
       along Valley Street southbound from a point 137 feet south of Hixon Place to a
       point 130 feet south thereof. Stopping and standing is prohibited along Parker
       Avenue westbound from Valley Street to Academy Street and from Valley Street
       to the west side of the Erie-Lackawanna Railroad Overpass. In addition,
       stopping and standing is prohibited along Parker Avenue eastbound from
       Maplewood Avenue to Valley Street. Parking is prohibited along both sides of
       Parker Avenue between the hours of 9:00 A.M. to 11:00 A.M. and 1:00 P.M. to
       3:00 P.M. on school days unless those vehicles are owned by a resident parking
       in front of their residence, a contractor, service and delivery vehicle or an
       emergency vehicle as stated in the provisions of 257-18B. Parking along Parker
       Avenue eastbound is restricted to a time period of two hours between 8:00 A.M.
       and 4:00 P.M. on school days from Valley Street to Summit Avenue. Parking is
       prohibited in the area of the NJ Transit bus stops located along both sides of
       Valley Street immediately north of the intersection.

       The land use immediately adjacent to the intersection of Valley Street and Parker
       Avenue is basically business with the exception of Columbia High School, which
       is located in the northeast quadrant of the intersection. A Shell gas station
       occupies the southeast quadrant of the intersection and has access to both
       Valley Street and Parker Avenue.

       Traffic Analyses: Keller and Kirkpatrick, along with the Maplewood Township
       Engineer's Office, performed a classified manual turning movement count
       program for the signalized intersection of Valley Street and Parker Avenue on
       Wednesday, October 18, 2006 between the hours of 6:00 and 9:30 A.M., 11:00
       A.M. and 2:00 P.M., and 3:00 and 6:00 P.M. The count data was classified in 15-
       minute increments into the following categories:

       Pedestrians
       Automobiles / Light Trucks

       Heavy Trucks/Buses ­ tractor trailers

       Keller and Kirkpatrick summarized the count data in a traffic flow diagram, which
       indicates the AM, midday and PM peak hour turning volumes. A copy of the

       As indicated in the analyses, all of the approaches to the intersection operate at
       a LOS `C' or better during each of the peak hours with the exception of Parker
       Avenue westbound during the PM peak hour, which operates at a LOS `D'.
       During the PM peak hour queues reach lengths of over 300 feet along each of
       the approaches with the exception of Parker Avenue eastbound.

       Crash Analysis: Keller and Kirkpatrick obtained from the Maplewood Township
       Engineer's Office the individual crash reports for the three-year period extending
       from June 1, 2003 through to August 30, 2006 at the intersection of Valley Street
       and Parker Avenue and prepared collision diagrams summarizing the crashes for
       each year. According to the individual police reports four, twelve, twelve and
       seven crashes occurred at this intersection during the years of 2003, 2004, 2005
       and 2006, respectively. Of note are the pedestrian crashes, two in 2005 and one
       in 2006, which occurred at the intersection. In the four years analyzed, seven
       crashes were same direction side swipe collisions, most were a result of vehicles
       passing on the right through the intersection. A high percentage of the crashes
       that occurred at the intersection are rear end type collisions, which is typical for
       signalized intersections. A copy of the collision diagrams can found in Appendix
       D.

       Conceptual Solution: The following lists the recommended improvements to the
       intersection of Valley Street and Parker Avenue to address the need for traffic
       calming measures and/or traffic safety improvements as identified in the traffic
       survey for this intersection. Input received at the February 22, 2007 field meeting
       with the Maplewood Township Engineer was incorporated, where appropriate, in
       the recommended improvements.

       ¾ Install bump-outs in the northwest and northeast quadrants. The bump-outs
         will reduce the potential for motorists to pass left turning vehicles on the right,
         reduce pedestrian crossing distances, increase visibility for and of the


Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                          20
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



           pedestrian, increase intersection sight distance, and may reduce travel
           speeds along Valley Street.
       ¾   Replace the existing traffic signal trombone mast arms and eight-inch signal
           heads with standard traffic signal mast arms and twelve-inch signal heads to
           increase signal visibility and thereby potentially reduce the number of rear
           end crashes.
       ¾   Install countdown pedestrian signals in all four quadrants to increase
           pedestrian expectancy.
       ¾   Install a ground mounted R10-11A, No Turn on Red, sign along the
           westbound approach and R10-11A, No Turn on Red, signs on the each of the
           traffic signal mast arms along the northbound, southbound and eastbound
           approaches to the intersection.
       ¾   Install a pedestrian crosswalk along with the appropriate pedestrian warning
           signs across the southbound approach of Valley Street at its' intersection with
           Cromwell Place. The pedestrian crosswalk should include "ladder" striping to
           increase its visibility to motorists.
       ¾   Relocate the existing NJ Transit bus stop located in the northwest quadrant to
           accommodate the proposed bump-out.

       An additional option is the installation of a raised intersection to increase visibility
       for and of the pedestrians.

       The estimated construction cost for the conceptual solution, without the raised
       intersection, is $66,100, which does not include any costs associated with the
       acquisition of right of way, potential drainage modifications or utility impacts. A
       copy of the construction cost estimate can be found in Appendix E.

       A plan copy of the conceptual solution for this intersection can be found in
       Appendix F.


   2. Elmwood Avenue and Prospect Street: The traffic survey identified turning
      difficulties from Elmwood Avenue, which forms the stem of this `T' intersection,
      onto Prospect Street due to speeding along Prospect Street and limited
      intersection sight distance. In addition, it was identified as a hazardous crossing
      for pedestrians.

       Existing Conditions: Prospect Street in the vicinity of Elmwood Avenue is an
       undivided two-lane urban collector running south to north with an approximate
       roadway width of 36 feet. Elmwood Avenue in the vicinity of Prospect Street is
       an undivided two-lane urban collector running west to east with an approximate
       roadway width of 34 feet. Both roadways have a posted speed limit of 25 mph
       and are under the jurisdiction of Maplewood Township.

       Prospect Street and Elmwood Avenue form a `T' type intersection with Elmwood
       Avenue serving as the stem of the `T'. The northbound approach of Prospect

       Avenue is residential. The majority of the residences within the project area have
       off-street parking.

       According to AASHTO ­ A Policy on Geometric De sign of Highways and Streets
       ­ 2004, departure sight triangles for intersections with stop control should be
       provided from the minor road for turns onto or for crossing the major road. The
       vertex (decision point) of the departure sight triangle on the minor road is 14-½
       feet from the edge of the traveled way for the major road. This represents the
       typical position of the minor road driver's eye when a vehicle is stopped relatively
       close to the major road. Based on a design speed of 30 mph and a height of 3-½
       feet for the driver's eye and the approaching vehicle, the intersection sight
       distance from the minor road along the major road for a passenger vehicle
       turning left from the minor road is 335 feet and the intersection sight distance for
       a passenger vehicle turning right onto the major road or crossing the major road
       is 290 feet according to AASHTO.

Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                   22
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County




       According to the Maplewood Township Zoning and Development Regulations
       sight triangles are required at each corner of an intersection of streets. The sight
       triangle is defined as that area bounded by the intersecting street lines and a
       straight line connecting sight points located on each of the two intersecting street
       lines with lengths based on the streets functional classification, which was
       determined from the Township's Master Plan.

       The required sight triangles according to both AASHTO and the Maplewood
       Township Zoning and Development Regulations along with a digital image of
       each sight triangle has been overlaid on an aerial plan titled "Sight Triangle Plan
       ­ Prospect Street and Elmwood Avenue" which can be found in Appendix G. As
       can be seen on this plan, vegetation within the northeast quadrant restricts the
       intersection sight distance. In addition, based on field observation the vertical
       curvature along Prospect Street south of Elmwood Avenue may also restrict
       intersection sight distance. However, a survey of the centerline profile along
       Prospect Street should be performed to verify the same.

       Crash Analysis: Collision diagrams were not prepared for the intersection of
       Prospect Street and Elmwood Avenue. However, according to the traffic survey
       as compiled by the Maplewood Township Engineer, nine (9) reported crashes
       occurred at this intersection between January 1, 2001 and December 31, 2004.

       Conceptual Solution: The following lists the recommended improvements to the
       intersection of Prospect Street and Elmwood Avenue to address the need for
       traffic calming measures and/or traffic safety improvements as identified in the
       traffic survey for this intersection. Input received at the February 22, 2007 field
       meeting with the Maplewood Township Engineer was incorporated, where
       appropriate, in the recommended improvements.

       ¾ Install bump-outs in the northwest, northeast and southeast quadrants of the
         intersection. The bump-outs will reduce pedestrian crossing distances,
         increase visibility for and of the pedestrian, increase intersection sight
         distance, and may reduce travel speeds along Prospect Street.
       ¾ Clear the sight triangles, as specified in both AASHTO and the Maplewood
         Township Zoning and Development Regulations, of all obstructions, as
         reasonable.
       ¾ Install W11-2, Pedestrian Crossing, signs in advance of and at the
         intersection along both approaches of Prospect Street.
       ¾ Insert "ladder" striping within the crosswalks to increase its visibility to
         motorists.

       Additional options include the installation of speed cushions or chicanes along
       Prospect Street both north and south of Elmwood Avenue.




Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                   23
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       The estimated construction cost for the conceptual solution, without the speed
       cushions or chicanes, is $10,300, which does not include any costs associated
       with the acquisition of right of way, potential drainage modifications or utility
       impacts. A copy of the construction cost estimate can be found in Appendix E.

       A plan copy of the conceptual solution for this intersection can be found in
       Appendix F.

       Jefferson Avenue.

       Jefferson Avenue and Valley Street is a mixture of business and residential. The
       majority of the businesses and residences within the project area have off-street
       parking. Columbia High School is located along Valley Street approximately 600
       feet north of Jefferson Avenue.

       According to AASHTO the intersection sight distance from the minor road along
       the major road for a passenger vehicle turning left from the minor road based on
       a design speed of 40 mph is 445 feet and the intersection sight distance for a
       passenger vehicle turning right onto the major road or crossing the major road is
       385 feet.

       Appendix G. As can be seen on this plan, the intersection sight distance from
       Jefferson Avenue is restricted by vegetation and structures within the sight
       triangles, especially by vegetation in the northwest quadrant of the intersection.

       appropriate, in the recommended improvements.

         will increase intersection sight distance, increase visibility for and of the
         pedestrian, and may reduce travel speeds along Ridgewood Road.
       ¾ Clear the sight triangles, as specified in both AASHTO and the Maplewood
         Township Zoning and Development Regulations, of all obstructions, as
         reasonable.
       ¾ Eliminate on-street parking along Ridgewood Road northbound within the
         limits of the sight triangles.
       ¾ Widen the existing sidewalk in front of the local businesses located along
         Ridgewood Road northbound, north of Cedar Lane to discourage parking.

       Additional options include the installation of speed cushions or chicanes along
       Ridgewood Road south of Cedar Lane/E. Cedar Lane.

       The estimated construction cost for the conceptual solution, without the speed
       cushions or chicanes, is $12,300, which does not include any costs associated

       with the acquisition of right of way, potential drainage modifications or utility

IV. ROADWAYS

   1. Ridgewood Road: The traffic survey identified speeding, limited intersection
      sight distance at unspecified locations, and significant pedestrian traffic as
      hazards along this roadway.

       Existing Conditions: Ridgewood Road within the Township of Maplewood is an
       undivided two-lane urban collector running south to north with an approximate
       average roadway width of 30 feet. It has an approximate overall length of 1.3
       miles within the Township with numerous unsignalized intersections and a
       flashing signal at Baker Street. According to the NJDOT Straight-Line Diagram
       for Ridgewood Road, the municipal boundary between Maplewood Township and
       Millburn Township is located approximately 50 feet south of Bailey Road and the
       municipal boundary between Maplewood Township and South Orange Township
       is located approximately 150 feet north of Cedar Lane/E. Cedar Lane.
       Ridgewood Road has a posted speed limit of 25 mph and is under the jurisdiction
       of Maplewood Township.

       According to Maplewood Township ordinance, no vehicle shall be parked on any
       thoroughfare or public parking lot between the hours of 2:00 A.M. and 6:00 A.M.
       Parking is prohibited at all times along Ridgewood Road southbound from the
       municipality boundary between Maplewood Township and South Orange
       Township to New England Road. Parking is also prohibited along Ridgewood
       Road northbound from Curtis Place to Lenox Place and from Woodland Road to
       a point 45 feet south. Stopping and standing is prohibited along Ridgewood
       Road southbound from the municipal boundary between Maplewood Township
       and South Orange Township to Durand Road, and along Ridgewood Road
       northbound from Maryland Road to Virginia Road and from Woodland Avenue to
       a point 82 feet north thereof.

       The land use along Ridgewood Road is basically residential with a few small
       business located in the vicinity of Cedar Lane/E. Cedar Lane. The majority of the
       residences and businesses within the project area have off-street parking. In
       addition, Jefferson Elementary School is located along Ridgewood Road at its
       intersection with Virginia Road.

       Conceptual Solutions: The following summarizes two conceptual solutions
       which address the need for traffic calming measures and/or traffic safety
       improvements as identified in the traffic survey for Ridgewood Road. Input

Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       received at the February 22, 2007 field meeting with the Maplewood Township
       Engineer was incorporated, where appropriate, in the recommended
       improvements.

       ¾ Install bump-outs at the following intersecting roadways with Ridgewood

           Cedar Lane/E. Cedar Lane                Woodland Road

           Euclid Avenue                           Maple Terrace
           Maryland Road                           Cottage Court
           Durand Road                             Berkeley Road
           Roosevelt Road

         The bump-outs will increase intersection sight distance, increase visibility for
         and of the pedestrian, and may reduce travel speeds along Ridgewood Road.
       ¾ Provide "ladder" striping within each crosswalk so as to increase their visibility
         to motorists.
       ¾ Clear the sight triangles, as specified in both AASHTO and the Maplewood
         Township Zoning and Development Regulations, of all obstructions, as
         reasonable, for each intersection along Ridgewood Road.

       If the bump-outs do not achieve the desired result with respect to reducing travel
       speeds along Ridgewood Road over a predetermined time frame then speed
       cushions could be installed in areas identified with excessive speeding.

       The estimated construction cost for Conceptual Solution No. 1 is $352,000,
       which does not include any costs associated with the acquisition of right of way,
       potential drainage modifications or utility impacts. A copy of the construction cost
       estimate can be found in Appendix E.

       Conceptual Solution No. 2:
       ¾ Install raised intersections at the following intersecting roadways with
         Ridgewood Road.

Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



           New England Road
           Collinwood Road
           Claremont Avenue
           Euclid Road
           Durand Road
           Baker Street
           Ridgewood Terrace

           Carlton Court
           Maple Terrace
           Berkeley Road

         The raised intersections will increase visibility for and of the pedestrian and
         will reduce travel speeds along Ridgewood Road. However, they do not
         address the concerns regarding limited intersection sight distance.
       ¾ Provide "ladder" striping within each crosswalk and clear the sight triangles as
         recommended in Conceptual Solution No. 1.

       The estimated construction cost for Conceptual Solution No. 2 is $132,000,
       which does not include any costs associated with the acquisition of right of way,
       potential drainage modifications or utility impacts. A copy of the construction cost
       estimate can be found in Appendix E.

       The following traffic calming measures were deemed inappropriate for this
       roadway as noted:

       ¾ On-Street Parking or a Bicycle Lane, both of which are a form of roadway
         narrowing, provide a perception of enclosure to the motorist, and in turn
         reduce speed. However, since off-street parking is readily available the
         demand for on-street parking may not be adequate to provide a constant
         stimulus. The striping for the parking stalls and/or a bicycle lane alone will not
         provide adequate stimuli to significantly treduce travel speeds.
       ¾ Chicanes were deemed inappropriate due to the close proximity of the side
         streets with one another.
       ¾ Center Islands would restrict access to and from driveways from one
         direction.    In addition, currently available on-street parking would be
         eliminated and thereby result in a net increase in travel speeds.
       ¾ Traffic Circles were deemed inappropriate due to the close proximity of
         intersecting side streets at various locations along Ridgewood Road and
         since they would most likely require the relocation of existing crosswalks to
         locations with lower driver expectancy.

       Conceptual Solution No. 1 is our recommended alternative since it addresses
       each of the issues identified in the traffic survey.

Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                   36
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



         reduce speed. However, since off-street parking is readily available the
         demand for on-street parking may not be adequate to provide a constant
         stimulus. The striping for the parking stalls and/or a bicycle lane alone will not
         provide adequate stimuli to significantly reduce travel speeds.
       ¾ Chicanes were deemed inappropriate due to the close proximity of the side
         streets with one another and the existing non-tangential horizontal alignment.
       ¾ Center Islands would restrict access to and from driveways from one

         eliminated and thereby result in a net increase in travel speeds.
       ¾ Traffic Circles were deemed inappropriate since they would most likely
         require the relocation of existing crosswalks to locations with lower driver
         expectancy. In addition, traffic circles would most likely require modifications
         to the existing curblines, which may require additional right-of-way and would
         impact the residences in the vicinity of the intersections.

       Conceptual Solution No. 1 is our recommended alternative since it addresses the
       issue of excessive speeding as identified in the traffic survey and increases
       pedestrian safety.

       within the project limits by narrowing the roadway with a center island, essentially
       creating a boulevard effect. The roadway width would be narrowed to provide a
       12-foot wide travel lane on either side of the center island, as shown in Figure 6.

       refuge for pedestrians crossing the street. In addition, "ladder" striping within
       each crosswalk should be provided as recommended in Conceptual Solution No.
       1. It was assumed that the pavement design for Prospect Street was continuous
       for the cross section of roadway. The quality of the drainage system along

       shifted closer to the curbline.

   4. Baker Street: The traffic survey identified speeding, limited visibility resulting
      from the overpass and significant pedestrian traffic as hazards along this
      roadway.

       Existing Conditions: Baker Street is an undivided two-lane local road running
       east to west with an approximate roadway width of 40 feet. It has an
       approximate overall length of 0.5 miles with four unsignalized intersections and a
       traffic signal at the intersection of Baker Street and Valley Street. The studied
       limits extend along Baker Street between Valley Street and Maplewood Avenue.
       Baker Street has a posted speed limit of 25 mph and is under the jurisdiction of
       Maplewood Township.

       According to Maplewood Township ordinance, no vehicle shall be parked on any
       thoroughfare or public parking lot between the hours of 2:00 A.M. and 6:00 A.M.
       Parking is prohibited at all times along Baker Street westbound from Valley
       Street to a point 270 feet west thereof. Parking is prohibited at all times along
       Baker Street eastbound from Valley Street to a point 250 feet west thereof and
       from the Erie-Lackawanna Railroad to Maplewood Avenue. Parking along Baker
       Street westbound is restricted to a time period of one hour between 9:00 A.M.
       and 7:00 P.M. everyday from the Erie-Lackawanna Railroad to Maplewood
       Avenue. Parking along Baker Street westbound is restricted to a time period of
       thirty minutes at all times from Burnet Street to a point 190 feet northwest
       thereof. Parking along Baker Street eastbound is restricted to a time period of


Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                   40
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       three hours between 8:00 A.M. and 4:00 P.M. everyday from Burnet Street to a
       point 255 feet west thereof. Parking along Baker Street westbound is restricted
       to a time period of fifteen minutes at all times from Dunnell Road to a point 160
       feet east thereof.

       The land use along Baker Street is basically recreational. Memorial Park and the
       Maplewood Country Club are located along Baker Street as well as Maplewood
       Middle School and the Maplewood Memorial Library.

       Conceptual Solutions: The following summarizes the conceptual solution which
       addresses the need for traffic calming measures and/or traffic safety
       improvements as identified in the traffic survey for Baker Street. Input received
       at the February 22, 2007 field meeting with the Maplewood Township Engineer
       was incorporated, where appropriate, in the recommended improvements.

       The conceptual solution proposes to reduce speeding and improve pedestrian
       safety by installing a bump-out at the intersection of Baker Street and Burnett
       Street. Mid-block narrowing would be installed along Baker Street in the vicinity
       of Dunnell Road. The bump-out and mid-block narrowing would reduce the
       crossing time for pedestrians while improving their visibility to motorists. In
       addition, it is recommended that the pedestrian crosswalks include "ladder"
       striping to increase their visibility to motorists. It is also recommended that a
       speed study be performed along Baker Street to determine the effectiveness of
       speed cushions.

       The estimated construction cost for the Conceptual Solution is $23,000, which
       does not include any costs associated with right of way acquisition or utility
       impacts. A copy of the construction cost estimate can be found in Appendix E.

       The following traffic calming measures were deemed inappropriate for this
       roadway as noted:

       ¾ Chicanes were deemed inappropriate due to the existing non-tangential
         horizontal alignment along Baker Street.
       ¾ Center Islands would restrict access to and from driveways from one
         direction.    In addition, currently available on-street parking would be
         eliminated and thereby result in a net increase in travel speeds.
       ¾ Traffic Circles were deemed inappropriate since they would most likely
         require the relocation of existing crosswalks to locations with lower driver
         expectancy and would eliminate much of the existing on-street parking along
         Baker Street.

       roadway as noted:

       ¾ On-Street Parking or a Bicycle Lane, both of which are a form of roadway
         narrowing, provide a perception of enclosure to the motorist, and in turn
         reduce speed. However, since on-street parking is readily available the
         demand for on-street parking may not be adequate to provide a constant
         stimulus. The striping for the parking stalls and/or a bicycle lane alone will not
         provide adequate stimuli to significantly reduce travel speeds.
       ¾ Chicanes were deemed inappropriate due to the close proximity of the
         residential driveways with one another and the short length of roadway
         studied.
       ¾ Center Islands would restrict access to and from driveways from one
         direction.   In addition, currently available on-street parking would be
         eliminated and thereby result in a net increase in travel speeds.
       ¾ Traffic Circles were deemed inappropriate since they would most likely
         require modifications to the existing curblines, which may require additional
         right-of-way and would impact the residences in the vicinity of the
         intersections.

      as a bypass route between Valley Street and Prospect Street/Springfield Avenue.

       The land use along Sommer Avenue, Hubert Place, Osborne Terrace and Girard
       Place is basically residential. The majority of the single-family residences within
       the project area have off-street parking. Tuscan Elementary School is located
       along Harvard Avenue between Valley Street and Osborne Terrace, which is
       immediately north of this collection of roadways.

       Conceptual Solutions: The following summarizes two conceptual solutions
       which addresses the need for traffic calming measures and/or traffic safety
       improvements as identified in the traffic survey for Sommer Avenue. Input
       received at the February 22, 2007 field meeting with the Maplewood Township
       Engineer was incorporated, where appropriate, in the recommended
       improvements.

       Conceptual Solution No. 1 proposes to construct traffic circles along Sommer
       Avenue at its intersections with Hubert Place and Osborne Terrace and speed
       cushions along Girard Place between Valley Street and Hubert Place and Hubert
       Place and Osborne Terrace. This would reduce speeding and deter vehicles
       from using these roadways as bypasses since vehicles would be forced to slow
       down along each roadway. The traffic circles would include pavers or other
       treatment to create a mountable curb for larger vehicles.

       The estimated construction cost for the Conceptual Solution No. 1 is $32,000,
       which does not include any costs associated with right of way acquisition or utility
       impacts. A copy of the construction cost estimate can be found in Appendix E.

       Conceptual Solution No. 2:
       ¾ Install raised intersections at the following locations:

           Sommer Avenue and Hubert Place
           Sommer Avenue and Osborne Terrace
           Girard Place and Hubert Place
           Girard Place and Osborne Terrace

       The raised intersections will increase visibility for and of the pedestrian, reduce
       travel speeds, and may deter motorists from using this collection of roadways as
       a bypass.

       The estimated construction cost for the Conceptual Solution No. 2 is $54,000,
       which does not include any costs associated with right of way acquisition or utility
       impacts. A copy of the construction cost estimate can be found in Appendix E.

       The following traffic calming measures were deemed inappropriate for this
       roadway as noted:




Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                   44
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       ¾ On-Street Parking or a Bicycle Lane, both of which are a form of roadway
         narrowing, provide a perception of enclosure to the motorist, and in turn
         reduce speed. However, since off-street parking is readily available the
         demand for on-street parking may not be adequate to provide a constant
         stimulus. The striping for the parking stalls and/or a bicycle lane alone will not
         provide adequate stimuli to significantly reduce travel speeds or deter
         motorists from using this collection of roadways as a bypass route.
       ¾ Chicanes were deemed inappropriate due to the close proximity of the
         driveways.
       ¾ Center Islands would restrict access to and from driveways from one
         direction.   In addition, currently available on-street parking would be
         eliminated and thereby result in a net increase in travel speeds.

       Conceptual Solution No. 1 is our recommended alternative since it addresses the
       issue of excessive speeding and bypass traffic as identified in the traffic survey.

      Terrace due to Columbia High School.

       lane while the westbound approach consists of a left/through lane and one

       According to Maplewood Township ordinance, no vehicle shall be parked on any
       thoroughfare or public parking lot between the hours of 2:00 A.M. and 6:00 A.M.

       along Kensington Terrace northbound is restricted to a time period of two hours
       between 8:00 A.M. and 4:00 P.M. on school days from Oakland Road to Crowell
       Place. Parking along Kensington Terrace southbound is restricted to a time
       period of two hours between 8:00 A.M. and 4:00 P.M. on school days from
       Oakland Road to Parker Avenue.

       Stopping and standing is prohibited along Parker Avenue westbound from Valley
       Street to Academy Street. Parking is prohibited along both sides of Parker
       Avenue between the hours of 9:00 A.M. to 11:00 A.M. and 1:00 P.M. to 3:00 P.M.
       on school days unless those vehicles are owned by a resident parking in front of
       their residence, a contractor, service and delivery vehicle or an emergency
       vehicle as stated in the provisions of 257-18B. Parking along Parker Avenue
       eastbound is restricted to a time period of two hours between 8:00 A.M. and 4:00
       P.M. on school days from Valley Street to Summit Avenue.

       The land use in the vicinity of Parker Avenue and Kensington Terrace is basically
       residential. The majority of the single-family residences within the project area
       have off-street parking. Columbia High School is immediately adjacent to the
       intersection of Parker Avenue and Kensington Terrace.

       Conceptual Solutions: The following summarizes two conceptual solutions
       which addresses the need for traffic calming measures and/or traffic safety
       improvements as identified in the traffic survey for Kensington Terrace. Input
       received at the February 22, 2007 field meeting with the Maplewood Township
       Engineer was incorporated, where appropriate, in the recommended
       improvements.

       Conceptual Solution No. 1 proposes to construct a traffic circle at the
       intersection of Kensington Terrace and Edgewood Place and speed cushions
       along Kensington Terrace between Parker Avenue and Crowell Place, Crowell
       Place and Edgewood Place, Edgewood Place and Lincoln Place, and Lincoln
       Place and Oakland Road. This would reduce speeding and deter vehicles from
       using this roadway as a bypass since vehicles would be forced to slow down
       along the roadway. In addition, the traffic circle would include pavers or other
       treatment to create a mountable curb for larger vehicles.

       The estimated construction cost for the Conceptual Solution No. 1 is $37,000,
       which does not include any costs associated with right of way acquisition or utility
       impacts. A copy of the construction cost estimate can be found in Appendix E.

       Conceptual Solution No. 2:
       ¾ Install raised intersections at the following intersecting roadways with
         Kensington Terrace.

           Crowell Place
           Edgewood Place
           Lincoln Place

       The estimated construction cost for the Conceptual Solution No. 2 is $39,000,
       which does not include any costs associated with right of way acquisition or utility
       impacts. A copy of the construction cost estimate can be found in Appendix E.

       In addition to Conceptual Solution Nos. 1 and 2, the following lists the
       recommended improvements to the intersection of Parker Avenue and
       Kensington Terrace to address the significant pedestrian volume:

       ¾ Install bump-outs in the southwest and southeast quadrants and along the
         north side of Parker Avenue adjacent to Columbia High School. The bump-
         outs will, reduce pedestrian crossing distances, increase visibility for and of
         the pedestrian and may reduce travel speeds along Parker Avenue.
       ¾ The pedestrian crosswalks should include "ladder" striping as recommended
         in Conceptual Solution No. 1.

       The estimated construction cost for the intersection of Parker Avenue and
       Kensington Terrace is $10,800, which does not include any costs associated with



Keller & Kirkpatrick
August 2007                                                                                   48
Traffic Calming Needs Study ­ Township of Maplewood, Essex County



       right of way acquisition or utility impacts.          A copy of the construction cost
       estimate can be found in Appendix E.

       A plan copy of the Conceptual Solution for this intersection can be found in
       Appendix F.

       The following traffic calming measures were deemed inappropriate for this
       roadway as noted:

       ¾ On-Street Parking or a Bicycle Lane, both of which are a form of roadway
         narrowing, provide a perception of enclosure to the motorist, and in turn
         reduce speed. However, off-street parking is readily available and residents
         along this roadway expressed concern over high school students loitering in
         the area should parking be permitted. The striping for the parking stalls
         and/or a bicycle lane alone will not provide adequate stimuli to significantly
         reduce travel speeds or deter motorists from using this roadway as a bypass
         route.
       ¾ Chicanes were deemed inappropriate due to the close proximity of the
         residential driveways with one another and the short length of roadway
         studied.
       ¾ Center Islands would restrict access to and from driveways from one
         direction.   In addition, currently available on-street parking would be
         eliminated and thereby result in a net increase in travel speeds.

       Conceptual Solution No. 1 is our recommended alternative along with the
       improvements at the intersection of Parker Avenue and Kensington Terrace
       since it addresses the issue of excessive speeding as identified in the traffic
       survey and increases pedestrian safety. This solution will also discourage the
       use of this roadway as a bypass route.
